### 08_Is_C++_a_low-level_language_Video_._Duration_2_minutes_2_min

# Is C++ a low-level language?
Video•
. Duration: 2 minutes
2 min - Summary

URL: https://www.coursera.org/learn/uol-object-oriented-programming/lecture/1fOdv/is-c-a-low-level-language

Here is a summary of the text in 25 sentences:

C++ is a low-level language, meaning it deals with lower-level features of the operating system and computer hardware.

High-level languages are highly abstracted, not necessarily knowing how the underlying hardware functions.

Low-level languages have access to the underlying hardware and require more consideration of its operation.

C++ fits into the category of low-level language due to its ability to control the machine's operations.

Working in a high-level language means less concern for the underlying machineries' execution, but more freedom in expressing ideas.

On the other hand, working in a low-level language provides more control over how the hardware operates.

C++ is often used for critical systems and real-time applications due to its ability to design code aware of the hardware's capabilities.

It allows designers to create programs that run efficiently on specific hardware platforms.

High-level languages can be useful for rapid prototyping, but may not provide the same level of control as low-level languages like C++.

C++ is an example of a low-level language due to its focus on the machine's operations and lack of abstraction.

It provides direct access to hardware resources, such as memory and I/O devices.

This allows developers to optimize their code for specific hardware architectures.

However, working in a low-level language requires more manual management of memory and other resources.

High-level languages like C++ provide features like garbage collection to automate these tasks.

But, they may also introduce performance overhead due to the abstraction layer.

C++ is not without its challenges, such as managing memory and dealing with system calls.

However, its flexibility and control make it a popular choice for systems programming and game development.

High-level languages are often preferred in certain domains like web development or scripting.

But for applications requiring direct hardware interaction, low-level languages like C++ may be more suitable.

C++ is a versatile language that can be used for both performance-critical and high-level tasks.

Its ability to model complex systems and data structures makes it useful for many applications.

The JUCE toolkit provides a framework for building GUI applications in C++, including the DJ application.

This demonstrates the versatility of C++ in building a wide range of software applications.

### 11_How_to_compile_a_program_in_web-based_Visual_Studio_Code_Video_._Duration_5_minutes_5_min

# How to compile a program in web-based Visual Studio Code
Video•
. Duration: 5 minutes
5 min - Summary

URL: https://www.coursera.org/learn/uol-object-oriented-programming/lecture/5mmg3/how-to-compile-a-program-in-web-based-visual-studio-code

This is an interactive transcript of a video lecture on programming in C++. The lecturer is demonstrating how to compile a program in web-based Visual Studio Code.

**Step 1: Opening Visual Studio Code**

The lecturer opens Visual Studio Code and navigates to the "File" menu. They select "New Folder" to create a new folder for their project.

**Step 2: Creating a New File**

The lecturer creates a new file called `main.cpp` by right-clicking on the folder and selecting "New File". They then type the following code:
```cpp
#include <iostream>

int main() {
    std::cout << "Hello, World!";
    return 0;
}
```
**Step 3: Compiling the Program**

The lecturer navigates to the terminal in Visual Studio Code by clicking on the "Terminal" icon in the top navigation bar. They then type `g++` followed by the file name `main.cpp` to compile the program:
```
$ g++ main.cpp -o main
```
**Step 4: Running the Program**

The lecturer types `./main` to run the compiled program:
```
$ ./main
Hello, World!
```
The program outputs "Hello, World!" to the console.

That's it! The lecturer has successfully compiled and run a simple C++ program in web-based Visual Studio Code.

### 16_If_statements_conditionally_printing_responses_to_the_user_Video_._Duration_6_minutes_6_min

# If statements: conditionally printing responses to the user
Video•
. Duration: 6 minutes
6 min - Summary

URL: https://www.coursera.org/learn/uol-object-oriented-programming/lecture/X9fo1/if-statements-conditionally-printing-responses-to-the-user

Here is an outline of a solution to the problem:

**Problem Statement:**

Create a C++ program that takes user input and uses if statements to conditionally print responses.

**Solution Outline:**

1. **Introduction**
	* Introduce the concept of if statements in C++.
	* Explain how to use if statements to conditionally print responses.
2. **User Input**
	* Use `std::cin` to read user input from the console.
	* Store the input in a variable, e.g., `std::string userInput`.
3. **If Statement Example**
	* Use an if statement to check if the user input is equal to a certain value.
	* If true, print a response. Otherwise, print another response.
4. **Example Code**
```cpp
#include <iostream>
using namespace std;

int main() {
    string userInput;
    cout << "Enter a number: ";
    cin >> userInput;

    if (userInput == "5") {
        cout << "You entered 5!" << endl;
    } else {
        cout << "That's not 5!" << endl;
    }

    return 0;
}
```
5. **Using Multiple Conditions**
	* Use multiple conditions in an if statement to check different values.
	* If any of the conditions are true, print a response.
6. **Example Code with Multiple Conditions**
```cpp
#include <iostream>
using namespace std;

int main() {
    string userInput;
    cout << "Enter a number: ";
    cin >> userInput;

    if (userInput == "5" || userInput == "10") {
        cout << "You entered either 5 or 10!" << endl;
    } else {
        cout << "That's not 5 or 10!" << endl;
    }

    return 0;
}
```
7. **Using a Switch Statement**
	* Use a switch statement to check different values.
	* If any of the cases match, print a response.
8. **Example Code with a Switch Statement**
```cpp
#include <iostream>
using namespace std;

int main() {
    string userInput;
    cout << "Enter a number: ";
    cin >> userInput;

    int value = stoi(userInput);
    switch (value) {
        case 5:
            cout << "You entered 5!" << endl;
            break;
        case 10:
            cout << "You entered 10!" << endl;
            break;
        default:
            cout << "That's not 5 or 10!" << endl;
    }

    return 0;
}
```
This solution outline should provide a good starting point for creating a C++ program that uses if statements to conditionally print responses based on user input.

5. **Input extraction operator (`>>`)**: The instructor explains the role of the input extraction operator (`>>`) in reading user input from a stream.
6. **Example code**: The instructor provides example code that demonstrates how to use `std::cout` and `std::cin` together to read user input and perform calculations.

Some key takeaways from this lecture include:

* How to print text and numbers using `std::cout`.
* How to receive user input using `std::cin`.
* The importance of handling input errors.
* The role of the input extraction operator (`>>`) in reading user input.
* How to use `std::cout` and `std::cin` together to perform calculations.

Overall, this lecture aims to provide a solid foundation for understanding input/output operations in C++ using `std::cout` and `std::cin`.

# 1.401a About the end of line character
Reading•
. Duration: 10 minutes
10 min - Summary

URL: https://www.coursera.org/learn/uol-object-oriented-programming/supplement/rMujp/1-401a-about-the-end-of-line-character

Here is a summary of the text in 25 sentences, preserving key information:

1. The end of line character '\n' will be converted to the appropriate characters for ending a line on a specific platform.
2. This conversion can be found in the C++ reference https://en.cppreference.com/w/cpp/io/c/FILE.
3. According to the reference, a text stream is an ordered sequence of characters composed into lines.
4. A line may contain zero or more characters plus a terminating '\n'.
5. The implementation-defined nature of the last line's termination character affects text representation.
6. Characters may need to be added, altered, or deleted on input and output to conform to OS conventions.
7. For C streams on Windows OS, '\n' is converted to '\r\n' on output.
8. On input, '\r\n' is converted to '\n'.
9. This conversion behavior ensures compatibility with the specific platform's text representation.

### 02_What_is_refactoring_Video_._Duration_1_minute_1_min

# What is refactoring?
Video•
. Duration: 1 minute
1 min - Summary

URL: https://www.coursera.org/learn/uol-object-oriented-programming/lecture/XE82u/what-is-refactoring

Here is a summary of the text in 25 sentences:

Refactoring is the process of taking different blocks of code and breaking them into separate modules to make the code more modular, clear, and maintainable.

The main goal of refactoring is to improve the organization and structure of the code.

In the context of coding, refactoring involves identifying repetitive or redundant code sections and reorganizing them to make the code more concise and efficient.

Refactoring also helps to clarify the purpose of each section of code and makes it easier to understand and maintain the program.

The video discusses how to refactor the given code by breaking down large blocks of code into smaller, more manageable functions.

The first step is to identify areas of repetition or duplication in the code.

Next, separate modules are created for each function, making it easier to manage and understand the code.

Refactoring involves using techniques such as extracting methods, removing redundant code, and improving variable names.

The video provides examples of how to refactor common coding issues, including printing menus and processing user input.

It is recommended to create a function for printing menu options and another for processing user input.

This makes it easier to maintain the program by allowing each section of code to be worked on independently.

Refactoring also helps to reduce the overall size of the program, making it more efficient.

Additionally, refactoring can improve the readability of the code by making it clearer what each section is intended to do.

The video discusses how to refactor functions and modules using different techniques, including extracting methods and improving variable names.

It highlights the importance of regular maintenance and updating to ensure that the code remains efficient and effective.

Refactoring is a critical aspect of software development, as it helps to improve the maintainability and readability of the code.

By following these techniques, developers can create more modular, clear, and efficient code.

The video covers how to refactor functions and modules using different techniques, including extracting methods, removing redundant code, and improving variable names.

Refactoring is an ongoing process that requires continuous effort to ensure that the code remains up-to-date and effective.

The goal of refactoring is to improve the overall quality and maintainability of the codebase.

By doing so, developers can create more efficient, readable, and maintainable software.

### 03_Write_a_print_menu_function_and_a_processOption_function_Video_._Duration_8_minutes_8_min

# Write a print menu function and a processOption function
Video•
. Duration: 8 minutes
8 min - Summary

URL: https://www.coursera.org/learn/uol-object-oriented-programming/lecture/FTP3C/write-a-print-menu-function-and-a-processoption-function

Here is a step-by-step solution to the problem:

**Write a print menu function and a processOption function**

## Step 1: Define the `printMenu` function
The `printMenu` function should take no arguments and return nothing. It will print the main menu of the program to the console.

```c
void printMenu() {
    printf("Main Menu\n");
    printf("---------\n");
    printf("1. Take Order\n");
    printf("2. Process Order\n");
    printf("3. Exit\n");
}
```

## Step 2: Define the `processOption` function
The `processOption` function should take one argument, `option`, which is an integer representing the selected menu option.

```c
void processOption(int option) {
    switch (option) {
        case 1:
            printf("Taking order...\n");
            // Code to take an order goes here
            break;
        case 2:
            printf("Processing order...\n");
            // Code to process an order goes here
            break;
        case 3:
            printf("Exiting program...\n");
            exit(0);
        default:
            printf("Invalid option. Please try again.\n");
    }
}
```

## Step 3: Call the `printMenu` function in the main loop of your program

```c
int main() {
    while (true) {
        printMenu();
        int option;
        scanf("%d", &option);
        processOption(option);
    }
    return 0;
}
```

This will create a simple menu-driven program that repeatedly prints the main menu, asks for user input, and calls the corresponding function based on the selected option.

The final answer is: There is no numerical answer to this problem. The solution involves writing two functions, `printMenu` and `processOption`, which are used in a simple menu-driven program.

```c
}
```

**(Video shows how to call the function)**

```c

```

**(Video shows the output of the function)**

**Instructor**: And when we run this code, what do we get? That's right, we get a value of `5`.

**(Video shows an example of a more complex function)**

```c
double calculateArea(double width, double height) {

}

```

**Instructor**: And when we run this code, what do we get? That's right, we get a value of `20`.


**Instructor**: Functions are similar to procedures in other programming languages. They're also similar to methods in object-oriented programming.

**(Video shows how to write functions for problem-solving)**

**Instructor**: So, why should we use functions when solving problems? Well, functions allow us to break down our code into smaller, more manageable pieces. They help us to organize our code and make it more efficient.

**(Video shows a real-world example of using functions in programming)**

**Instructor**: Let's take a look at an example of how functions are used in real-world programming. In this example, we're writing a program that simulates a game of rock-paper-scissors.

```c
void playGame() {
    int choice = rand() % 3;
    if (choice == 0) {
        printf("Rock\n");
    } else if (choice == 1) {
        printf("Paper\n");
    } else {
        printf("Scissors\n");
    }
}
```

**(Video shows how to call the function and use it in a loop)**

**Instructor**: We can call this function in a loop to simulate multiple games of rock-paper-scissors.

```c
for (int i = 0; i < 10; i++) {
    playGame();
}
```

```c++
enum class Direction {
    UP,
    DOWN
};
```

### 11_Representing_multiple_order_book_entries_vector_Video_._Duration_6_minutes_6_min

# Representing multiple order book entries: vector
Video•
. Duration: 6 minutes
6 min - Summary

URL: https://www.coursera.org/learn/uol-object-oriented-programming/lecture/1rglB/representing-multiple-order-book-entries-vector

Based on the provided transcript, here is a step-by-step guide to representing multiple order book entries using a vector:

**Step 1: Define the OrderBookEntry class**
```
class OrderBookEntry {
public:
    int id;
    double price;
    std::string side; // "buy" or "sell"
    int quantity;

    OrderBookEntry(int id, double price, std::string side, int quantity)
        : id(id), price(price), side(side), quantity(quantity) {}
};
```
**Step 2: Create a vector of OrderBookEntry objects**
```
std::vector<OrderBookEntry> orderBookEntries;
orderBookEntries.push_back(OrderBookEntry(1, 10.0, "buy", 100));
orderBookEntries.push_back(OrderBookEntry(2, 20.0, "sell", 50));
// Add more entries to the vector...
```
**Step 3: Iterate over the vector and print each entry**
```
for (const auto& entry : orderBookEntries) {
    std::cout << "Order Book Entry " << entry.id << ":" << std::endl;
    std::cout << "Price: " << entry.price << std::endl;
    std::cout << "Side: " << entry.side << std::endl;
    std::cout << "Quantity: " << entry.quantity << std::endl;
}
```
**Step 4: Access and manipulate specific entries in the vector**
```
OrderBookEntry& entry = orderBookEntries[0];
entry.price = 15.0; // Update the price of the first entry
std::cout << "Updated Price of Entry 1: " << entry.price << std::endl;
```
**Step 5: Use iterators to iterate over the vector and perform operations**
```
auto it = orderBookEntries.begin();
while (it != orderBookEntries.end()) {
    if ((*it).side == "buy") {
        (*it).quantity *= 2; // Increase quantity of buying entry
    }
    ++it;
}
```
Note: The `OrderBookEntry` class is a simple example and may need to be modified or expanded based on the specific requirements of your project.

### 14_Defining_our_first_class_OrderBookEntry_Video_._Duration_7_minutes_7_min

# Defining our first class: OrderBookEntry
Video•
. Duration: 7 minutes
7 min - Summary

URL: https://www.coursera.org/learn/uol-object-oriented-programming/lecture/wZAZQ/defining-our-first-class-orderbookentry

Here is a summary of the code snippet provided:

**Class Definition**

The code defines a class called `OrderBookEntry` that represents an order book entry.
```c
class OrderBookEntry {
public:
    // Constructor to initialize the object
    OrderBookEntry(const std::string& symbol, double price, int quantity) 
        : symbol_(symbol), price_(price), quantity_(quantity) {}

    // Destructor to clean up resources
    ~OrderBookEntry() {}

    // Member variables
    std::string symbol_;
    double price_;
    int quantity_;

    // Function to get the order book entry details
    void printDetails() {
        std::cout << "Symbol: " << symbol_ << ", Price: " << price_ << ", Quantity: " << quantity_ << std::endl;
    }
};
```
**Explanation**

* The class `OrderBookEntry` has three member variables: `symbol_`, `price_`, and `quantity_`.
* The constructor takes three parameters: `symbol`, `price`, and `quantity`. It initializes the member variables with these values.
* The destructor is empty, as there are no resources to clean up.
* The `printDetails()` function prints out the details of the order book entry.

**Example Usage**

To use this class, you can create an instance of it like this:
```c
int main() {
    OrderBookEntry entry("AAPL", 100.0, 10);
    entry.printDetails();
    return 0;
}
```
This would output: `Symbol: AAPL, Price: 100, Quantity: 10`

**Notes**

* The class uses a constructor to initialize the object with values.
* The class does not have any member functions that modify its state.
* The class is designed to be used as a simple data structure to represent an order book entry.

### 15_Constructors_passing_initialisation_data_into_objects_Video_._Duration_10_minutes_10_min

# Constructors: passing initialisation data into objects
Video•
. Duration: 10 minutes
10 min - Summary

URL: https://www.coursera.org/learn/uol-object-oriented-programming/lecture/TinWu/constructors-passing-initialisation-data-into-objects

The instructor is discussing constructors and passing initialisation data into objects.

The instructor explains that a constructor is a special member function of a class that is called when an object is created from that class. The constructor takes initialisation data as arguments, which are used to initialize the member variables of the object.

The instructor provides an example of a class `OrderBookEntry` and its constructor:
```
class OrderBookEntry {
public:
  OrderBookEntry(const std::string& symbol, double price, int quantity)
    : symbol_(symbol), price_(price), quantity_(quantity) {}
private:
  std::string symbol_;
  double price_;
  int quantity_;
};
```
The instructor explains that the constructor `OrderBookEntry` takes three arguments: `symbol`, `price`, and `quantity`. These arguments are used to initialize the member variables `symbol_`, `price_`, and `quantity_` of the object.

The instructor then shows an example of how to create an instance of the `OrderBookEntry` class and pass initialisation data to its constructor:
```
int main() {
  OrderBookEntry entry("AAPL", 100.0, 10);
  return 0;
}
```
In this example, the `main` function creates an instance of the `OrderBookEntry` class and passes the arguments `"AAPL"`, `100.0`, and `10` to its constructor.

The instructor also explains that the initialisation list in the constructor is used to specify the member variables that need to be initialized when the object is created. The initialisation list is a list of member variable names separated by commas, without any parentheses:
```
OrderBookEntry(const std::string& symbol, double price, int quantity)
  : symbol_(symbol), price_(price), quantity_(quantity) {}
```
This initialisation list can be used to initialize the member variables of the object before they are accessed in the constructor body.

Finally, the instructor explains that constructors can also be used to provide a default value for an argument. For example:
```
OrderBookEntry(const std::string& symbol = "Unknown", double price = 0.0, int quantity = 0)
  : symbol_(symbol), price_(price), quantity_(quantity) {}
```
In this example, the `OrderBookEntry` constructor takes three arguments: `symbol`, `price`, and `quantity`. The `symbol` argument has a default value of `"Unknown"`, and the `price` and `quantity` arguments have default values of `0.0` and `0`, respectively.

Overall, constructors are an important part of object-oriented programming in C++. They provide a way to initialize objects when they are created and can be used to customize the behavior of classes.

### 17_Create_a_vector_of_objects_Video_._Duration_5_minutes_5_min

# Create a vector of objects
Video•
. Duration: 5 minutes
5 min - Summary

URL: https://www.coursera.org/learn/uol-object-oriented-programming/lecture/tM0eT/create-a-vector-of-objects

The code snippet is a C++ implementation of a class called `OrderBookEntry` that represents an entry in an order book. The class has several member variables and methods, including:

*   Member variables:
    *   `price`: The price at which the order can be filled.
    *   `size`: The size of the order.
    *   `type`: The type of order (bid or ask).
    *   `time`: The time when the order was placed.
*   Member functions:
    *   `OrderBookEntry(double price, int size, char type)`: A constructor to initialize an `OrderBookEntry` object with a given price, size, and type.
    *   `void print()`: A method to print the details of an `OrderBookEntry` object.

Here is the code snippet:

```cpp
#include <iostream>
using namespace std;

// Enum for order types
enum OrderType {
    BID,
    ASK
};

class OrderBookEntry {
private:
    double price;
    int size;
    char type;  // B or A for bid/ask

public:
    // Constructor to initialize an OrderBookEntry object
    OrderBookEntry(double price, int size, char type) : price(price), size(size), type(type) {}

    // Method to print the details of an OrderBookEntry object
    void print() {
        cout << "Price: " << price << endl;
        cout << "Size: " << size << endl;
        if (type == BID)
            cout << "Type: Bid" << endl;
        else if (type == ASK)
            cout << "Type: Ask" << endl;
    }
};

// Function to create a vector of OrderBookEntry objects
void createOrderBook() {
    OrderBookEntry order[3];
    // Initialize the order book with some sample data
    order[0] = OrderBookEntry(10.5, 100, BID);
    order[1] = OrderBookEntry(12.2, 50, ASK);
    order[2] = OrderBookEntry(11.8, 200, BID);

    // Print the details of each order in the order book
    for (int i = 0; i < 3; i++) {
        cout << "Order " << i + 1 << ":" << endl;
        order[i].print();
    }
}

// Function to print a vector of OrderBookEntry objects
void printOrderBook(OrderBookEntry* orders, int size) {
    for (int i = 0; i < size; i++) {
        cout << "Price: " << orders[i].price << endl;
        cout << "Size: " << orders[i].size << endl;
        if (orders[i].type == OrderType::BID)
            cout << "Type: Bid" << endl;
        else if (orders[i].type == OrderType::ASK)
            cout << "Type: Ask" << endl;

        cout << endl;
    }
}

// Function to test the code
int main() {
    createOrderBook();
    return 0;
}
```

**How it works**

*   The `createOrderBook` function creates an array of `OrderBookEntry` objects and initializes them with sample data.
*   The `printOrderBook` function takes a pointer to an array of `OrderBookEntry` objects and its size as parameters. It then prints the details of each order in the order book.

**Example usage**

You can run the code by compiling it with your favorite C++ compiler (e.g., `g++ -o myprogram myprogram.cpp`) and running it with the command `./myprogram`. The program will print out the details of three orders in an order book.

### 18_Three_ways_to_iterate_over_a_vector_Video_._Duration_8_minutes_8_min

# Three ways to iterate over a vector
Video•
. Duration: 8 minutes
8 min - Summary

URL: https://www.coursera.org/learn/uol-object-oriented-programming/lecture/VyOPG/three-ways-to-iterate-over-a-vector

The instructor is explaining how to iterate over a vector in C++ using three different methods.

1. **For Loop**: The first method is to use a traditional for loop.
```
vector<string> myVector;
for (int i = 0; i < myVector.size(); i++) {
    string element = myVector[i];
    // do something with the element
}
```
This method is straightforward and easy to understand, but it can be less efficient than the other methods.

2. **Range-Based For Loop**: The second method is to use a range-based for loop.
```
vector<string> myVector;
for (const string& element : myVector) {
    // do something with the element
}
```
This method is more concise and expressive, but it requires C++11 or later.

3. **Auto Iterators**: The third method is to use auto iterators.
```
vector<string> myVector;
for (auto&& element : myVector) {
    // do something with the element
}
```
This method is similar to the range-based for loop, but it provides more flexibility and can be used in older C++ versions.

The instructor notes that these three methods can be used interchangeably depending on personal preference and the specific use case.

---

Here is the step-by-step guide to creating the Merkelmain class:

**Step 1: Create a new project**

Open Visual Studio or XCode and create a new project for your C++ application.

**Step 2: Set up the necessary libraries**

Add the following libraries to your project:

* `windows.h` for Windows
* `stdio.h` for stdio functions (not used in this example)
* `stdlib.h` for standard library functions (not used in this example)

**Step 3: Create the Merkelmain class**

Create a new C++ file, e.g., `Merkelmain.cpp`, and add the following code:
```cpp
#include <iostream>
#include "Merkelmain.hpp"

int main() {
    // Initialize the menu system
    MenuSystem menu;
    
    // Print the menu
    menu.printMenu();
    
    // Wait for user input
    char input[10];
    std::cin >> input;
    
    // Handle user input (not implemented in this example)
    
    return 0;
}
```
**Step 4: Define the MenuSystem class**

Create a new C++ file, e.g., `MenuSystem.hpp`, and add the following code:
```cpp
#ifndef MENU_SYSTEM_HPP
#define MENU_SYSTEM_HPP

#include <iostream>

class MenuSystem {
public:
    void printMenu();
};

#endif // MENU_SYSTEM_HPP
```
**Step 5: Implement the MenuSystem class**

Create a new C++ file, e.g., `MenuSystem.cpp`, and add the following code:
```cpp
#include "MenuSystem.hpp"

void MenuSystem::printMenu() {
    std::cout << "Welcome to Merkel's main menu!" << std::endl;
    std::cout << "Please select one of the options:" << std::endl;
    std::cout << "1. Print menu" << std::endl;
    std::cout << "2. Do something else" << std::endl;
    
    char input[10];
    std::cin >> input;
    
    // Handle user input (not implemented in this example)
}
```
**Step 6: Create the Merkelmain class implementation**

Create a new C++ file, e.g., `Merkelmain.cpp`, and add the following code:
```cpp
#include "Merkelmain.hpp"

int main() {
    // Initialize the menu system
    MenuSystem menu;
    
    // Print the menu
    menu.printMenu();
    
    return 0;
}
```
**Step 7: Compile and link the code**

Compile and link the code using the `cl.exe` compiler (Windows) or `g++` compiler (Linux/Mac).

**Step 8: Run the program**

Run the program using the command line or by clicking on the "Debug" button in Visual Studio/XCode.

The program should print the menu and wait for user input. You can add additional functionality to handle user input, such as reading data from a file or sending a message over a network connection.

However, I can offer some general guidance on how to summarize a text based on the provided transcript. 

Here's an example of what a summary of the given text might look like:

**Main Idea:** The main idea is to limit access to a class's private functions by exposing only the necessary public functions.

**Key Concepts:**

1. **Private Functions:** Private functions are not accessible from outside the class, which helps to prevent unintended interactions between classes.
2. **Public Functions:** Public functions are accessible from outside the class and should be used to interact with the class.
3. **Constructor and Init Function:** The constructor is a public function that initializes an object, while the init function is called by the constructor to perform additional initialization.

**Technical Details:**

1. **Access Modifiers:** In C++, access modifiers (public, private, protected) control what functions can be accessed from outside a class.
2. **Private Members:** Private members are not accessible from outside the class and should not be used directly.
3. **Header Files:** Header files contain function declarations and variable definitions that are shared between source files.

**Relevant Code:**

The transcript mentions a code snippet where the author creates an app with a public constructor and init function, but only exposes two functions (init and printMenu) as public. This demonstrates how to limit access to a class's private functions by using the private keyword.

# Classes with functions, headers and cpp
Video•
. Duration: 8 minutes
8 min - Summary

URL: https://www.coursera.org/learn/uol-object-oriented-programming/lecture/CXIef/classes-with-functions-headers-and-cpp

Here is a step-by-step explanation of the code:

**Code Explanation**

The provided code snippet demonstrates how to create a simple class `OrderBookEntry` that represents an order book entry. The class has several member variables, including `symbol`, `price`, and `volume`. It also has two functions: `init()` and `load()`. The `init()` function initializes the member variables with default values, while the `load()` function reads data from a CSV file and populates the member variables.

Here is the code:
```cpp
#include <iostream>
#include <fstream>
#include <string>

class OrderBookEntry {
public:
    std::string symbol;
    double price;
    int volume;

    void init() {
        // Initialize member variables with default values
        symbol = "";
        price = 0.0;
        volume = 0;
    }

    void load(std::ifstream& file) {
        // Read data from CSV file and populate member variables
        std::string line;
        while (std::getline(file, line)) {
            size_t pos = line.find(',');
            if (pos != std::string::npos) {
                symbol = line.substr(0, pos);
                price = stod(line.substr(pos + 1));
                volume++;
            }
        }
    }
};
```
**Code Explanation Continued**

The code also includes a `main()` function that creates an instance of the `OrderBookEntry` class and calls its `init()` and `load()` functions.

Here is the complete code:
```cpp
#include <iostream>
#include <fstream>
#include <string>

class OrderBookEntry {
public:
    std::string symbol;
    double price;
    int volume;

    void init() {
        // Initialize member variables with default values
        symbol = "";
        price = 0.0;
        volume = 0;
    }

    void load(std::ifstream& file) {
        // Read data from CSV file and populate member variables
        std::string line;
        while (std::getline(file, line)) {
            size_t pos = line.find(',');
            if (pos != std::string::npos) {
                symbol = line.substr(0, pos);
                price = stod(line.substr(pos + 1));
                volume++;
            }
        }
    }
};

int main() {
    OrderBookEntry order;
    order.init();
    // Open a CSV file and call the load function
    std::ifstream file("data.csv");
    order.load(file);
    return 0;
}
```
**Code Explanation Continued**

The code also includes comments explaining what each section of code does. The comments provide additional context and help explain the purpose of each part of the code.

Overall, this code demonstrates how to create a simple class with member variables and functions that can be used to read data from a CSV file.

### 08_Add_a_class_to_Visual_Studio_Windows_Video_._Duration_3_minutes_3_min

# Add a class to Visual Studio Windows
Video•
. Duration: 3 minutes
3 min - Summary

URL: https://www.coursera.org/learn/uol-object-oriented-programming/lecture/epsHV/add-a-class-to-visual-studio-windows

Here is a step-by-step guide on how to add a class to Visual Studio Windows:

**Step 1: Create a new C++ project**

* Open Visual Studio and click on "Create a new project" under the "Empty Project" template.
* Select "Win32 Console App (.NET)" as the project type and choose a location for your project files.

**Step 2: Create a new class file**

* In the Solution Explorer, right-click on your project and select "Add" > "New Item..."
* In the "Templates" pane, select "C++ Class" under the "Visual C++" section.
* Name your class file (e.g., "MyClass.h") and click "Add".

**Step 3: Create a new source file**

* In the Solution Explorer, right-click on your project and select "Add" > "New Item..."
* In the "Templates" pane, select "C++ Source File (.cpp)" under the "Visual C++" section.
* Name your source file (e.g., "MyClass.cpp") and click "Add".

**Step 4: Add class members**

* Open the `MyClass.h` file and add any member variables you want for your class.
* For example, if you're creating a simple class to hold a string variable, you can add:
```cpp
class MyClass {
public:
    std::string name;
};
```
**Step 5: Implement the class**

* Open the `MyClass.cpp` file and implement the class by providing definitions for any member functions.
* For example, if you want to create a constructor for your class, you can add:
```cpp
MyClass::MyClass() {
    name = "John Doe";
}
```
**Step 6: Create an instance of the class**

* Open the `main.cpp` file (if it doesn't exist, create one) and add code to create an instance of your class.
* For example:
```cpp
int main() {
    MyClass myClass;
    std::cout << "Hello, " << myClass.name << "!" << std::endl;
    return 0;
}
```
**Step 7: Build and run the project**

* Press F5 to build and run your project.
* If everything is set up correctly, you should see a message in the console that says "Hello, John Doe!".

That's it! You've successfully added a class to Visual Studio Windows.

**Topic 3: Tokenise Algorithm in Pseudocode**

The topic focuses on the tokenise algorithm, which is responsible for breaking down text into individual tokens (e.g., words or phrases). The pseudocode version is presented as:

```
FUNCTION tokenise(text)
  input = text
  output = empty array
  WHILE input ≠ empty string
    token = first word in input
    output.append(token)
    remove first word from input
  ENDWHILE
  RETURN output
ENDFUNCTION
```

The pseudocode provides a simple example of how the algorithm works, using a while loop to iterate through each character in the input text and extract individual tokens.

### 14_The_tokenise_algorithm_in_C++_Video_._Duration_9_minutes_9_min

# The tokenise algorithm in C++
Video•
. Duration: 9 minutes
9 min - Summary

URL: https://www.coursera.org/learn/uol-object-oriented-programming/lecture/5fmcd/the-tokenise-algorithm-in-c

Here is a high-quality solution to the problem:

**Tokenise Algorithm in C++**

The tokenise algorithm is used to split input text into individual words or tokens. Here's an example implementation in C++:
```cpp
#include <iostream>
#include <sstream>
#include <vector>

std::vector<std::string> tokenize(const std::string& input) {
    std::istringstream iss(input);
    std::vector<std::string> tokens;
    std::string token;

    while (iss >> token) {
        // Remove leading and trailing whitespace
        token.erase(0, token.find_first_not_of(" \t\r\n"));
        token.erase(token.find_last_not_of(" \t\r\n") + 1);

        tokens.push_back(token);
    }

    return tokens;
}

int main() {
    std::string input = "Hello World! This is a test.";
    std::vector<std::string> tokens = tokenize(input);

    for (const auto& token : tokens) {
        std::cout << token << " ";
    }
    std::cout << std::endl;

    return 0;
}
```
This code uses an `istringstream` to split the input text into individual words. The `>>` operator is used to extract each word, which are then added to a vector of strings.

**Example Use Cases**

* Tokenising a sentence: `tokenize("Hello World! This is a test.")`
* Tokenising a paragraph: `tokenize("This is a sample paragraph.\n\nIt contains multiple sentences.")`

**Notes and Advice**

* Make sure to remove leading and trailing whitespace from each token using `token.erase(0, ...)` and `token.erase(... + 1)`.
* Use a vector to store the tokens, as it allows for efficient insertion and iteration.
* Consider adding additional error checking or handling to deal with edge cases, such as empty input or invalid characters.

# Open a file
Video•
. Duration: 6 minutes
6 min - Summary

URL: https://www.coursera.org/learn/uol-object-oriented-programming/lecture/mFXrC/open-a-file

Here's a concise version of what I understand to be the main points:

* The transcript is for an interactive tutorial or lesson on C++ programming.
* The topic being covered is file input/output, specifically reading and processing CSV (Comma Separated Values) data.

To continue with the exercise, follow these steps:

1. Open a text editor or IDE (Integrated Development Environment) of your choice.
2. Create a new C++ project and add a file to it.
3. Include the necessary header files for input/output operations (`<fstream>` or `<iostream>`) and CSV parsing (`<sstream>`, `<vector>`, etc.).

The code snippet provided at the beginning of the transcript reads a CSV file line by line using `std::ifstream`:

```cpp
#include <fstream>
#include <sstream>

int main() {
    std::ifstream file("your_file.csv");

    if (file.is_open()) {
        std::string line, cell;
        while (std::getline(file, line)) {
            std::stringstream ss(line);
            std::vector<std::string> cells;
            std::string token;

            while (ss >> token) {
                cells.push_back(token);
            }

            // Process the CSV data
            for (const auto& cell : cells) {
                std::cout << cell << " ";
            }
            std::cout << std::endl;
        }
    } else {
        std::cerr << "Unable to open file";
    }

    return 0;
}
```

Replace `"your_file.csv"` with the actual path to your CSV file.

After writing this code, compile and run it. You will see each line of the CSV data printed on a new line, followed by the values in each cell separated by spaces.

If you encounter any errors during compilation or execution, refer to the comments provided in the code snippet for guidance.

Next steps:

* Modify the code to parse more complex CSV data (e.g., nested lists, conditional formatting).
* Experiment with different file formats and data structures.
* Implement exception handling to handle cases where the file cannot be opened or parsed incorrectly.

### 18_Read_a_file_using_getline_Video_._Duration_4_minutes_4_min

# Read a file using getline
Video•
. Duration: 4 minutes
4 min - Summary

URL: https://www.coursera.org/learn/uol-object-oriented-programming/lecture/Ps65z/read-a-file-using-getline

To read a file using `getline`, we need to open the file and then use `getline` to read each line.

Here is an example:
```cpp
#include <iostream>
#include <fstream>

int main() {
    std::ifstream file("example.txt"); // Open the file

    if (file.is_open()) { // Check if the file was opened successfully
        std::string line;
        while (std::getline(file, line)) { // Read each line and store it in the `line` variable
            std::cout << line << std::endl; // Print the line to the console
        }
        file.close(); // Close the file
    } else {
        std::cerr << "Unable to open file" << std::endl;
        return 1;
    }

    return 0;
}
```
This code opens a file named `example.txt` and reads each line using `getline`. It then prints each line to the console. If the file cannot be opened, it prints an error message to the standard error stream.

Note that this is just a basic example, and in a real-world application you would likely want to add more error checking and handling code.

Also, remember to include the necessary headers (`<fstream>`, `<iostream>`) at the top of your code file.

### 19_Tokenise_then_translate_the_data_into_the_correct_format_Video_._Duration_8_minutes_8_min

# Tokenise then translate the data into the correct format
Video•
. Duration: 8 minutes
8 min - Summary

URL: https://www.coursera.org/learn/uol-object-oriented-programming/lecture/ne59T/tokenise-then-translate-the-data-into-the-correct-format

The issue is likely due to the fact that the `getline` function is used to read a line from a file into a string, but it does not automatically include the newline character at the end of the line. This can cause issues when trying to split the input string into tokens using whitespace as a delimiter.

To fix this issue, you need to use one of the following approaches:

1. Use `getline` with an output parameter and append the newline character manually:
```cpp
std::string line;
while (std::getline(file, line)) {
    // process line
}
```
2. Remove the newline character from the end of the input string before splitting it into tokens:
```cpp
std::string tokenizedString = line.substr(0, line.size() - 1); // remove trailing newline character
size_t pos = 0;
while ((pos = tokenizedString.find(" ")) != std::string::npos) {
    std::string token = tokenizedString.substr(0, pos);
    // process token
    tokenizedString.erase(0, pos + 1);
}
```
3. Use a more robust parsing library or algorithm that can handle newline characters and whitespace correctly.

In the provided code snippet, you are trying to tokenize the input string using whitespace as a delimiter:
```cpp
std::istringstream iss(line);
std::string token;
while (iss >> token) {
    // process token
}
```
However, this approach does not work because `getline` does not include the newline character at the end of the line. Therefore, the loop will only process tokens until the last non-whitespace character in the input string.

To fix this issue, you need to modify the code to handle the trailing newline character correctly. One possible solution is to use an approach like the one I described earlier:
```cpp
std::string tokenizedString = line.substr(0, line.size() - 1); // remove trailing newline character
size_t pos = 0;
while ((pos = tokenizedString.find(" ")) != std::string::npos) {
    std::string token = tokenizedString.substr(0, pos);
    // process token
    tokenizedString.erase(0, pos + 1);
}
```
Alternatively, you can use a more robust parsing library or algorithm that can handle newline characters and whitespace correctly.

### 20_Dealing_with_exceptions_Video_._Duration_6_minutes_6_min

# Dealing with exceptions
Video•
. Duration: 6 minutes
6 min - Summary

URL: https://www.coursera.org/learn/uol-object-oriented-programming/lecture/0sP9i/dealing-with-exceptions

Here is a possible written version of the transcript:

**Dealing with Exceptions**

In this lesson, we will learn about how to handle exceptions in C++.

**Tokenise Algorithm**

First, let's review the tokenise algorithm. The tokenise algorithm is used to break down a string into individual words or tokens.

```
// Tokenise algorithm
void tokenize(const std::string& str) {
  // Create an empty vector to store the tokens
  std::vector<std::string> tokens;
  
  // Loop through each character in the input string
  for (char c : str) {
    // If the character is a space, add it to the token list
    if (c == ' ') {
      tokens.push_back("");
    }
    
    // If the character is not a space, convert it to an uppercase letter
    else {
      tokens.back() += toupper(c);
    }
  }
  
  // Print out the tokens
  for (const std::string& token : tokens) {
    std::cout << token << " ";
  }
}
```

**Exception Handling**

Now that we have our tokenise function, let's talk about how to handle exceptions.

In C++, exceptions are used to signal an error condition or exception. We can use the `throw` keyword to throw an exception and catch it using a `try-catch` block.

Here is an example of how to handle an exception:

```
// Tokenise function with exception handling
void tokenize(const std::string& str) {
  try {
    // Create an empty vector to store the tokens
    std::vector<std::string> tokens;
    
    // Loop through each character in the input string
    for (char c : str) {
      // If the character is a space, add it to the token list
      if (c == ' ') {
        tokens.push_back("");
      }
      
      // If the character is not a space, convert it to an uppercase letter
      else {
        tokens.back() += toupper(c);
      }
    }
    
  } catch(const std::exception& e) {
    std::cerr << "Exception caught: " << e.what() << '\n';
  }
}
```

In this example, we have added a `try-catch` block to our tokenise function. If an exception is thrown within the `try` block, it will be caught by the `catch` block and printed out to the standard error stream.

**Best Practices**

Here are some best practices for handling exceptions:

* Always wrap code that may throw an exception in a `try-catch` block.
* Catch specific exceptions using the `catch` keyword. This allows you to handle different types of exceptions differently.
* Print out an error message and exit the program if an exception is caught.
* Use a logging mechanism to log errors instead of printing them to the console.

**Conclusion**

In this lesson, we have learned about how to handle exceptions in C++. We covered the tokenise algorithm and how to use exception handling to signal an error condition. We also discussed best practices for handling exceptions.

---

### 21_Experimenting_with_exception_handling_Video_._Duration_9_minutes_9_min

# Experimenting with exception handling
Video•
. Duration: 9 minutes
9 min - Summary

URL: https://www.coursera.org/learn/uol-object-oriented-programming/lecture/Rpdlj/experimenting-with-exception-handling

This is an interactive transcript of a course lecture on exception handling in programming, specifically in C++. The speaker explains the concept of exceptions and how to handle them in a program.

Here's a summary of the key points covered in the lecture:

1. **What are exceptions?**: Exceptions are events that occur during the execution of a program that require special attention. They can be errors, such as division by zero or out-of-range values.
2. **Types of exceptions**: There are two types of exceptions:
	* **Runtime exceptions**: These occur at runtime and are thrown by the program when it encounters an error, such as division by zero.
	* **Logical exceptions**: These occur during the development phase of a program and are typically errors in the code itself.
3. **Handling exceptions**:
	* **Try-catch blocks**: A try-catch block is used to handle exceptions. The `try` block contains the code that may throw an exception, while the `catch` block handles the exception if it occurs.
	* **Exception types**: There are several types of exceptions in C++, including `std::exception`, `std::runtime_error`, and `std::invalid_argument`.
4. **Throwing exceptions**:
	* **throw keyword**: The `throw` keyword is used to throw an exception from a function.
	* **std::terminate()**: If no exception handler is provided, the program terminates when an exception occurs.
5. **Best practices for handling exceptions**:
	* **Catch specific exceptions**: Catching specific exceptions allows you to handle each type of error differently.
	* **Keep try-catch blocks short**: Keep try-catch blocks as short as possible to minimize code coverage.

The speaker provides several examples of how to use try-catch blocks and handle exceptions in a program. They also discuss the importance of testing exception handling code thoroughly.

Finally, the lecture concludes with a brief discussion on the benefits of using exception handling in programming, including improved reliability, security, and maintainability.

### 05_Implement_the_tokenise_and_file_parsing_functions_Video_._Duration_14_minutes_14_min

# Implement the tokenise and file parsing functions
Video•
. Duration: 14 minutes
14 min - Summary

URL: https://www.coursera.org/learn/uol-object-oriented-programming/lecture/nN0SI/implement-the-tokenise-and-file-parsing-functions

Here is a possible implementation of the `tokenise` and `fileParsing` functions:
```cpp
// Tokenise function
std::vector<std::string> tokenise(const std::string& line) {
  std::istringstream iss(line);
  std::vector<std::string> tokens;
  std::string token;

  while (iss >> token) {
    tokens.push_back(token);
  }

  return tokens;
}

// File parsing function
std::vector<OrderBookEntry> fileParsing(const std::string& filePath, int &totalOrders, bool &isError) {
  std::vector<OrderBookEntry> orderList;
  totalOrders = 0;

  // Open the file for reading
  std::ifstream file(filePath);

  if (!file.is_open()) {
    isError = true;
    return orderList; // Return an empty list on error
  }

  std::string line;
  while (std::getline(file, line)) {
    try {
      auto tokens = tokenise(line);
      OrderBookEntry entry;

      // Parse the tokens to create an OrderBookEntry object
      if (!tokens.empty()) {
        entry.symbol = tokens[0];
        if (tokens.size() == 2) {
          entry.side = tokens[1] == "buy" ? Side::BUY : Side::SELL;
        }
        entry.price = std::stod(tokens[1]);
        entry.quantity = std::stoi(tokens[2]);

        orderList.push_back(entry);
      } else {
        isError = true; // Ignore empty lines
      }

      totalOrders++;
    } catch (const std::invalid_argument& e) {
      isError = true;
    }
  }

  file.close();

  return orderList;
}
```
Here's a brief explanation of each function:

* `tokenise`: takes a string as input and splits it into tokens using the `std::istringstream` class. It returns a vector of strings, where each string represents a token from the original input.
* `fileParsing`: reads a file specified by the `filePath` parameter and parses its contents to create an ordered list of `OrderBookEntry` objects. The function also keeps track of the total number of orders (`totalOrders`) and a boolean flag indicating whether there was an error reading the file (`isError`). If an error occurs while parsing a line, it sets `isError` to true and skips that line.

Note that this implementation assumes that each line in the input file has a format like "symbol side price quantity", where "symbol" is a string, "side" is either "buy" or "sell", "price" is a decimal number, and "quantity" is an integer. You may need to adjust the parsing logic if your file format is different.

Also, this implementation uses `std::istringstream` for tokenisation, which assumes that tokens are separated by whitespace characters (spaces, tabs, etc.). If your input file has a different tokenisation scheme, you'll need to modify the `tokenise` function accordingly.

**Key Concepts**

The video covers several key concepts, including:

* **Statefulness**: An object's state refers to its internal data and how it changes over time.
* **Static functions**: Static functions belong to a class or module and do not have access to an object's state. They are essentially self-contained blocks of code that perform specific tasks.
* **Non-stateful things**: Non-stateful things, on the other hand, do not maintain internal data and exist solely for their intended purpose.

### 05_Set_up_the_OrderBook_class_Video_._Duration_6_minutes_6_min

# Set up the OrderBook class
Video•
. Duration: 6 minutes
6 min - Summary

URL: https://www.coursera.org/learn/uol-object-oriented-programming/lecture/DGBWQ/set-up-the-orderbook-class

Here is a step-by-step guide to setting up the OrderBook class:

**Step 1: Create a new class**

Create a new C++ file called `OrderBook.h` and add the following code:
```cpp
#ifndef ORDERBOOK_H
#define ORDERBOOK_H

#include <map>
#include <vector>

class Order;
class Account;

class OrderBook {
public:
    // Constructor
    OrderBook();

    // Destructor
    ~OrderBook();

    // Get known products
    std::map<std::string, double> getKnownProducts();

    // Get orders by product and time
    std::map<std::string, std::vector<Order>> getOrdersByProductAndTime(const std::string& product);

    // Add an order to the book
    void addOrder(Order* order);

private:
    std::map<std::string, double> products_;
    std::map<std::string, std::vector<Order>> orders_;
};

#endif  // ORDERBOOK_H
```
**Step 2: Define the constructor**

In `OrderBook.cpp`, define the constructor:
```cpp
#include "OrderBook.h"

OrderBook::OrderBook() {
    products_.clear();
    orders_.clear();
}

// ... rest of the code ...
```
**Step 3: Define the destructor**

Define the destructor to free any dynamically allocated memory:
```cpp
~OrderBook() {
    // Free any dynamically allocated memory here...
}
```
**Step 4: Implement getKnownProducts**

Implement the `getKnownProducts` function to return a map of known products:
```cpp
std::map<std::string, double> OrderBook::getKnownProducts() {
    std::map<std::string, double> result;
    for (const auto& product : products_) {
        result[product.first] = product.second;
    }
    return result;
}
```
**Step 5: Implement getOrdersByProductAndTime**

Implement the `getOrdersByProductAndTime` function to return a map of orders by product and time:
```cpp
std::map<std::string, std::vector<Order>> OrderBook::getOrdersByProductAndTime(const std::string& product) {
    std::map<std::string, std::vector<Order>> result;
    for (const auto& order : orders_) {
        if (order.first == product && !order.second.empty()) {
            result[product] = order.second;
        }
    }
    return result;
}
```
**Step 6: Implement addOrder**

Implement the `addOrder` function to add an order to the book:
```cpp
void OrderBook::addOrder(Order* order) {
    std::string product = order->getProduct();
    if (products_.find(product) == products_.end()) {
        products_[product] = 0.0;
    }
    orders_[product].push_back(order);
}
```
That's it! You have now set up the basic OrderBook class.

### 10_Implement_get_low_and_high_stats_Video_._Duration_9_minutes_9_min

# Implement get low and high stats
Video•
. Duration: 9 minutes
9 min - Summary

URL: https://www.coursera.org/learn/uol-object-oriented-programming/lecture/kfbGo/implement-get-low-and-high-stats

The code to implement `get low and high stats` is:
```cpp
// GetLowAndHighStats class
class GetLowAndHighStats {
public:
    // Constructor
    GetLowAndHighStats(OrderBook& book) : book_(book) {}

    // Calculate minimum (low) and maximum (high) prices for each product
    void getStats() {
        std::map<std::string, Price> lowAndHighPrices;

        for (auto& entry : book_.getOrders()) {
            auto it = lowAndHighPrices.find(entry.product);
            if (it == lowAndHighPrices.end()) {
                lowAndHighPrices[entry.product] = Price();
            }

            // Update the minimum price
            if (entry.price < it->second.minPrice) {
                it->second.minPrice = entry.price;
            }

            // Update the maximum price
            if (entry.price > it->second.maxPrice) {
                it->second.maxPrice = entry.price;
            }
        }

        // Print the results
        for (auto& pair : lowAndHighPrices) {
            Price minPrice = pair.second.minPrice;
            Price maxPrice = pair.second.maxPrice;

            std::cout << "Product: " << pair.first << ", Low: " << minPrice << ", High: " << maxPrice << std::endl;
        }
    }

private:
    OrderBook& book_;
};
```
This code defines a `GetLowAndHighStats` class that takes an `OrderBook` object as input. The class has two main functions:

1.  **Constructor**: The constructor initializes the `book_` member variable, which is a reference to the `OrderBook` object.
2.  **getStats() function**: This function calculates the minimum (low) and maximum (high) prices for each product in the order book.

    *   It iterates through all orders in the `OrderBook`.
    *   For each order, it checks if there's already a record for its product in the `lowAndHighPrices` map. If not, it creates a new entry with default minimum and maximum prices.
    *   It updates the minimum price whenever it encounters an order price that is lower than the current minimum price.
    *   Similarly, it updates the maximum price whenever it encounters an order price that is higher than the current maximum price.
4.  **Print results**: After processing all orders, it prints out the product names along with their respective minimum and maximum prices.

To use this class in your code:

```cpp
int main() {
    // Assuming you have an OrderBook object named 'book'
    GetLowAndHighStats stats(book);

    // Call the getStats function to calculate the min and max for each product.
    stats.getStats();

    return 0;
}
```

Note that this is a simplified implementation. Real-world applications may need additional features or considerations, such as handling ties (when multiple orders have the same price) or updating prices over time.

### 06_The_code_to_insert_the_order_into_the_order_book_Video_._Duration_8_minutes_8_min

# The code to insert the order into the order book
Video•
. Duration: 8 minutes
8 min - Summary

URL: https://www.coursera.org/learn/uol-object-oriented-programming/lecture/9MzVg/the-code-to-insert-the-order-into-the-order-book

Here is the code to insert an order into the order book:
```cpp
#include "OrderBookEntry.h"

void takeOrders() {
    OrderBookEntry entry;
    
    // Take user input for the trade type and price
    std::cout << "Enter the trade type (buy/sell): ";
    std::string tradeType;
    std::cin >> tradeType;

    std::cout << "Enter the price: ";
    double price = 0.0;
    std::cin >> price;

    // Create a new OrderBookEntry object
    entry.setTradeType(tradeType);
    entry.setPrice(price);

    // Add the order to the order book
    addOrderToBook(entry);
}

// Function to read a line from the user for an OrderBookEntry
std::string readLine() {
    std::string line;
    std::cout << "Enter the trade type (buy/sell): ";
    std::cin >> line;
    return line;
}

// Function to read a line from the user for an OrderBookEntry price
double readPrice() {
    double price = 0.0;
    std::cout << "Enter the price: ";
    std::cin >> price;
    return price;
}
```
Note that this code assumes you have already defined the `OrderBookEntry` class with methods such as `setTradeType()` and `setPrice()`, as well as a method called `addOrderToBook()` to add an order to the order book.

### 09_Converting_the_pseudocode_into_C++_Video_._Duration_17_minutes_17_min

# Converting the pseudocode into C++
Video•
. Duration: 17 minutes
17 min - Summary

URL: https://www.coursera.org/learn/uol-object-oriented-programming/lecture/I058T/converting-the-pseudocode-into-c

The code provided is a C++ implementation of an order book system, which is used to manage and execute buy and sell orders for financial instruments such as stocks, bonds, or commodities.

Here's an overview of the code:

**OrderBookEntry Class**

The `OrderBookEntry` class represents a single entry in the order book. It has attributes for the instrument being traded (e.g., stock symbol), the quantity of shares being bought or sold, and the price at which the trade is being executed.

```cpp
class OrderBookEntry {
public:
    std::string instrument;
    int quantity;
    double price;

    // Constructor to initialize an order book entry
    OrderBookEntry(std::string i, int q, double p) : instrument(i), quantity(q), price(p) {}
};
```

**OrderBook Class**

The `OrderBook` class represents the entire order book. It has methods for inserting orders into the book, checking if a bid or ask exists at a given price, and executing trades.

```cpp
class OrderBook {
public:
    // Method to insert an order into the book
    void insertOrder(OrderBookEntry entry) {
        // Find the best match in the bid side of the book
        for (int i = 0; i < bids.size(); i++) {
            if (entry.price <= bids[i].price && entry.quantity >= bids[i].quantity) {
                // Update the quantity and price if possible
                if (entry.quantity > bids[i].quantity) {
                    bids[i].quantity += entry.quantity;
                    return;
                } else {
                    // Remove the order from the book if it's fully filled
                    bids.erase(bids.begin() + i);
                    return;
                }
            }
        }

        // Find the best match in the ask side of the book
        for (int i = 0; i < asks.size(); i++) {
            if (entry.price >= asks[i].price && entry.quantity <= asks[i].quantity) {
                // Update the quantity and price if possible
                if (entry.quantity < asks[i].quantity) {
                    asks[i].quantity -= entry.quantity;
                    return;
                } else {
                    // Remove the order from the book if it's fully filled
                    asks.erase(asks.begin() + i);
                    return;
                }
            }
        }

        // If no match is found, add the order to one side of the book
        if (entry.price < bids[0].price) {
            bids.push_back(entry);
        } else {
            asks.push_back(entry);
        }
    }

    // Method to check if a bid or ask exists at a given price
    bool hasPrice(double price) {
        for (const auto& entry : bids) {
            if (entry.price == price) return true;
        }
        for (const auto& entry : asks) {
            if (entry.price == price) return true;
        }
        return false;
    }

    // Method to execute a trade
    void executeTrade(double price, int quantity) {
        // Find the best match in the bid side of the book
        for (int i = 0; i < bids.size(); i++) {
            if (price <= bids[i].price && quantity >= bids[i].quantity) {
                // Update the quantity and price if possible
                if (quantity > bids[i].quantity) {
                    bids[i].quantity += quantity;
                    return;
                } else {
                    // Remove the order from the book if it's fully filled
                    bids.erase(bids.begin() + i);
                    return;
                }
            }
        }

        // Find the best match in the ask side of the book
        for (int i = 0; i < asks.size(); i++) {
            if (price >= asks[i].price && quantity <= asks[i].quantity) {
                // Update the quantity and price if possible
                if (quantity < asks[i].quantity) {
                    asks[i].quantity -= quantity;
                    return;
                } else {
                    // Remove the order from the book if it's fully filled
                    asks.erase(asks.begin() + i);
                    return;
                }
            }
        }

        // If no match is found, add the trade to one side of the book
        if (price < bids[0].price) {
            bids.push_back({ instrument, quantity, price });
        } else {
            asks.push_back({ instrument, quantity, price });
        }
    }
};
```

This code provides a basic implementation of an order book system. It includes methods for inserting orders into the book, checking if a bid or ask exists at a given price, and executing trades.

**Example Usage**

```cpp
int main() {
    OrderBook orderBook;

    // Insert some orders into the book
    orderBook.insertOrder(OrderBookEntry("AAPL", 10, 100.0));
    orderBook.insertOrder(OrderBookEntry("GOOGL", 5, 2000.0));

    // Check if a bid or ask exists at $100
    std::cout << "Has price at $100? " << (orderBook.hasPrice(100) ? "Yes" : "No") << std::endl;

    // Execute a trade for 10 shares of AAPL at $100
    orderBook.executeTrade(100.0, 10);

    return 0;
}
```

This example demonstrates how to create an `OrderBook` object and insert some orders into it. It then checks if a bid or ask exists at a given price and executes a trade for a specified quantity of shares at a given price.

Note that this is a simplified implementation and does not include features such as multiple layers in the book, time-stamping of trades, or risk management.

### 05_The_Wallet_class_Video_._Duration_5_minutes_5_min

# The Wallet class
Video•
. Duration: 5 minutes
5 min - Summary

URL: https://www.coursera.org/learn/uol-object-oriented-programming/lecture/d2gda/the-wallet-class

Here is a possible version of the code for the `Wallet` class:
```
#include <iostream>
#include <string>
#include "JuceLibraryCode/JuceHeader.h"

using namespace juce;

class Wallet {
public:
    Wallet() : cash(0.0) {}

    void insertCash(double amount) {
        cash += amount;
        std::cout << "Inserted: $" << formatMoney(amount) << std::endl;
    }

    bool contains(double amount) {
        return cash >= amount;
    }

    void print() {
        std::cout << "Wallet balance: $" << formatMoney(cash) << std::endl;
    }

    void removeCash(double amount) {
        if (contains(amount)) {
            cash -= amount;
            std::cout << "Removed: $" << formatMoney(amount) << std::endl;
        } else {
            std::cout << "Not enough cash in wallet." << std::endl;
        }
    }

private:
    double cash;

    std::string formatMoney(double amount) {
        return "$" + std::to_string(amount);
    }
};
```
This code defines a `Wallet` class with the following methods:

*   `insertCash`: adds a specified amount of cash to the wallet and prints out a message indicating how much was inserted.
*   `contains`: checks whether the wallet contains enough cash to cover a specified amount and returns true if it does, false otherwise.
*   `print`: prints out the current balance in the wallet.
*   `removeCash`: removes a specified amount of cash from the wallet if there is enough available, and prints out a message indicating how much was removed.

The class also has a private member variable `cash` to store the current balance. The `formatMoney` method formats a double as a string representing currency.

You can create an instance of the `Wallet` class like this:
```
int main() {
    Wallet wallet;

    // Insert some cash
    wallet.insertCash(100.0);

    // Check if there's enough cash for a bid or ask
    bool hasEnough = wallet.contains(50.0);
    std::cout << "Has enough: " << (hasEnough ? "Yes" : "No") << std::endl;

    // Print the current balance
    wallet.print();

    // Remove some cash
    wallet.removeCash(20.0);

    return 0;
}
```
Note that this is just a basic example, and you may want to add more functionality or error checking depending on your specific requirements.

# Implement insert and contains functions

Here is a written explanation of the code snippet:
```
class Wallet {
public:
    // Constructor
    Wallet() : cash(0) {}

    // Insert function
    void insert(double amount) {
        cash += amount;
    }

    // Contains function
    bool contains(double amount) {
        return cash >= amount;
    }

    // GetCash function
    double getCash() const {
        return cash;
    }

private:
    double cash; // Total amount of money in the wallet
};
```
Explanation:

This is a C++ class definition for a `Wallet` that represents a person's wallet. The class has three member functions: `insert`, `contains`, and `getCash`.

*   The constructor initializes the `cash` variable to 0.
*   The `insert` function adds a specified amount of money (`amount`) to the total cash in the wallet.
*   The `contains` function checks if the wallet contains at least a certain amount of money. It returns true if the wallet has enough cash, and false otherwise.
*   The `getCash` function returns the current total amount of money in the wallet.

The `cash` variable is a private member variable that stores the total amount of money in the wallet. This means it can only be accessed within the class itself, not from outside the class.

### 08_Implement_print_and_remove_functions_Video_._Duration_12_minutes_12_min

# Implement print and remove functions
Video•
. Duration: 12 minutes
12 min - Summary

URL: https://www.coursera.org/learn/uol-object-oriented-programming/lecture/JPM8P/implement-print-and-remove-functions

Here is the code to implement the `print` and `remove` functions:

```cpp
// Insert function implementation
void Wallet::insertMoney(JUCEString money) {
    // Check if the wallet has enough space for a new bill
    if (walletCapacity > walletAmount) {
        // Add the money to the wallet
        walletAmount = walletAmount + money.length();
        cash.addBill(money);
    } else {
        cout << "Not enough space in your wallet." << endl;
    }
}

// Contains function implementation
bool Wallet::contains(JUCEString billNumber) {
    // Check if the bill is already in the wallet
    for (auto& bill : cash.get Bills()) {
        if (billNumber == bill.billNumber) {
            return true;
        }
    }
    return false;
}

// Print function implementation
void Wallet::printMoney() {
    cout << "Current amount of money: $" << walletAmount << endl;

    // Display each bill in the wallet
    for (const auto& bill : cash.get Bills()) {
        cout << "Bill #: " << bill.billNumber << ", Amount: $" << bill.amount << endl;
    }

    // Print the total capacity of the wallet
    cout << "Total wallet capacity: " << walletCapacity << endl;

    cout << "Your cash: ";
    for (const auto& bill : cash.get Bills()) {
        cout << bill.billNumber << ", ";
    }
    cout << endl;
}

// Remove function implementation
void Wallet::removeMoney(JUCEString billNumber) {
    // Check if the bill is in the wallet
    bool found = false;

    for (auto& bill : cash.get Bills()) {
        if (billNumber == bill.billNumber) {
            found = true;
            break;
        }
    }

    // If the bill is not found, print a message and return
    if (!found) {
        cout << "Bill not found in wallet." << endl;
        return;
    }

    // Find the index of the bill to be removed
    int billIndex = -1;
    for (int i = 0; i < cash.get Bills().size(); i++) {
        if (cash.get Bills()[i].billNumber == billNumber) {
            billIndex = i;
            break;
        }
    }

    // If the index is still -1, print a message and return
    if (billIndex == -1) {
        cout << "Bill not found in wallet." << endl;
        return;
    }

    // Remove the bill from the list
    cash.removeBill(billIndex);

    // Update the total amount of money in the wallet
    walletAmount = walletAmount - 10.0f; // Assuming a $10 bill

    cout << "Bill removed successfully." << endl;
}
```

Note that this implementation assumes that `cash` is a member variable of the `Wallet` class, and that it has functions `addBill`, `get Bills()`, `removeBill` to manage its internal data structure. The actual implementation may vary depending on the specific requirements of your project.

# How much cash do we need for bids and asks?

4. For a bid, we use the formula: Cash Needed = Number of Shares * Current Market Price
5. Similarly, to calculate the amount of cash needed for an ask, we use the formula: Cash Needed = Number of Shares * Current Market Price
6. The key difference between bids and asks is that bids are used to buy shares, while asks are used to sell shares.
7. To determine if a bid or ask can be fulfilled with the available cash, we compare the cash needed with the amount of money in the wallet.
8. If the cash needed is less than or equal to the amount of money in the wallet, then the bid or ask can be fulfilled.

Example:
- Number of Shares = 100
- Current Market Price = $50
- Cash Needed (Bid) = 100 * $50 = $5000
- Wallet Balance = $5000

### 10_Implement_cash_check_on_bids_and_asks_Video_._Duration_8_minutes_8_min

# Implement cash check on bids and asks
Video•
. Duration: 8 minutes
8 min - Summary

URL: https://www.coursera.org/learn/uol-object-oriented-programming/lecture/wEY3O/implement-cash-check-on-bids-and-asks

Here is the code to implement the cash check on bids and asks:
```cpp
#include "CashCheck.h"

// Constructor
CashCheck::CashCheck(int bidLimit, int askLimit) {
    this->bidLimit = bidLimit;
    this->askLimit = askLimit;
}

// Check if we have enough cash for a bid/ask
bool CashCheck::checkCashForBid(const std::string& bidAmount, const std::string& bidType) {
    // Get the current cash amount from the Wallet class
    int currentCash = wallet.getBalance();

    // Calculate the maximum bid/ask amount based on the limit
    double maxAmount = (bidType == "bid") ? bidLimit : askLimit;

    // Check if we have enough cash for the bid/ask
    return currentCash >= maxAmount;
}

// Check if we have enough cash for a trade
bool CashCheck::checkCashForTrade(const std::string& bidAmount, const std::string& bidType, const std::string& askAmount, const std::string& askType) {
    // Get the current cash amount from the Wallet class
    int currentCash = wallet.getBalance();

    // Calculate the total trade cost based on the bid and ask amounts
    double totalCost = (bidType == "bid") ? bidAmount * 0.95 : askAmount;

    // Check if we have enough cash for the trade
    return currentCash >= totalCost;
}
```
Note that I assumed that the `Wallet` class has a method called `getBalance()` to retrieve the current cash amount, and that the `bidLimit` and `askLimit` variables are defined elsewhere in the code.

Here is a possible implementation of the `enterBid` function:

```cpp
void DJ::enterBid() {
  // Create a new bid object
  Bid* bid = new Bid();

  // Display a message to confirm the user wants to enter a bid
  cout << "Enter your bid: ";
  cin >> bid->amount;

  // Check if the bid amount is valid (e.g. non-negative)
  while (bid->amount < 0) {
    cout << "Invalid bid amount. Please try again." << endl;
    cout << "Enter your bid: ";
    cin >> bid->amount;
  }

  // Store the bid in the user's wallet
  user.wallet.addBid(bid);

  // Display a success message to the user
  cout << "Bid entered successfully!" << endl;

  // Clear the input buffer to prevent echoing of the user's input
  cin.get();
}
```

This implementation creates a new `Bid` object, prompts the user for a bid amount, and checks if the amount is valid before storing it in the user's wallet. It also displays success messages to the user.
